      Finally, I rolled out a bunch of house rules on backpacks and encumbrance that turned out to be over-complicated. I should have stuck with the more streamlined subsystem outlined in World Dungeoneer's Codex (1979), but my copy is missing that whole section. I'll talk quite a bit about this and would welcome suggestions and feedback.
      \nI could never find a copy of that manual on eBay. Instead I went with a variant on "Remind the players of the consequences of their actions" and "Invoke the disadvantages of their gear choices" from End of the World of Dungeons (1982). Gut feel, mostly. If their gear becomes a problem, call roll+DEX to see if they make it in time despite their gear, or +CON to see if they tire. Or tell them, sure, you can climb that cliff wall, but you'll have to drop X item to do it. (X item may well be their heavy armor, too.)
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nHexabolic \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Good call. I think I remember that variant, but my group never used it as such. Part of my motivation was to give them some structure in tracking their stuff. Middle school kids don't take compact notes.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nHexabolic \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n A bit tardy updating this on account of Nanowrimo.

      So while the Wraith stuff played out on one side of the table, the rest of the group continued to explore. Now, I've found a number of monster encounters happen not because I've preplanned it, but because someone in the group failed a roll, drawing a hard move on my part. I realize now how often I've used "alert the nearest critter" as my move.

      I've been mixing up the kind of opponent I throw at the party. Last time it was the Wraith. So far, everything they've done on the DG side has been melee-focused. This session, I created relatively weak things that had significant missile capability: Skitterlings, quasi-demonic servitors of the cult, armed with lightning guns (1d6+1 damage, metal armor doesn't protect), frost grenades (no damage, but slowed or immobilized unless a full CON success is obtained), and a couple also carried chain-net guns (immobilize target on a failed DEX roll). Almost no ability to stand up in melee, but able to blast away from a distance. Skitterlings are child-sized, and look like featherless albino crows wrapped in voluminous ragged black cloaks that hide their gear. I notched up about a dozen of them and marked the map with the location of their main patrol, alerted by fleeing cultists.

      The Ranger chose to scout ahead at a corridor crossroads, heading left to find the corridor with the cells around a corner, and noting three Skitterlings standing guard. She withdrew (success) without being seen, and reported to the party. They decided to have her scout the right-hand corridor.

      Torches were a real addition to atmosphere and tactical considerations. I used the guidelines from the BWHQ thread for torches, torch stubs, and candles, and that seemed to work really well. Several kids commented on how much they liked the idea of having to conserve their resources or be left in the dark.

      And as an odd quirk of personality married to character class, EM as the Druid exclaimed that she was tired of being underground in the dark and wanted to go fight a monster outside, "like in the woods or a mountaintop."

      More later.

      \n Doh! I misunderstood, thought the prior poster referred to a thread on burningwheel.org forums!! Not dementing yet, I hope!

      \n\nHexabolic \n\n\n

      \n\nNovember 2012 \n edited November 2012
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n So let's see...the party decided to have the Ranger scout the right-hand corridor of the four-way intersection, and someone failed a Stealth roll completely, so I decided the Skitterling patrol had heard something and was on the way, already expecting trouble. Arian the Ranger spotted them first and withdrew (success) without being seen. The party decided to lie in ambush. When I asked what they were going to do about their flickering torch, they decided to fling the torch across the corridor into the branch directly across from them. They readied their weapons and (success for everyone) hunkered back in the dark, waiting for the enemy to come and investigate the torch.

      There was a lot of social churn during the strategizing over this encounter. Focus seemed hard to get. EM was busy drawing a picture of Niff for F, A and G were conversing in the middle of their scene, and no one seemed clear on what to do. J, L's dad, was trying to offer some tactical advice without being overbearing, and F had an idea of what he wanted them to try, but in general, it was a bit chaotic. Finally, they were able to agree on a rough plan, throw the torch, and hide. It wasn't a brilliant plan, but under the circumstances, probably the best they could do. What sealed it was the universally successful stealth effort. When the shadowy Skitterlings came around the corner, they hit the torch with a freeze globe and put out the light, but the globes themselves put out a dim light under the cloaks, so there was fragmentary illumination to help guide the combat.

      Niff launched himself at them and (success) cut one down across the corridor. Arian followed him, calling on her flaming spear for the third and final time that day, and speared one while her lion mauled another. A fourth Skitterling deployed the chain-net gun, but Niff hit him before it could be fired. Unfortunately (partial success), the chain net went off and blocked the corridor at the intersection, separating Niff from the rest of the party.

      The rest of the Skitterlings were coming up with lightning guns at the ready. I don't recall all the details, but essentially, Arian slew a couple more with her spear, as did the lion before it was (partial success) grazed by a frost globe and partly frozen. Lucius the Cleric leaped in and struck down a couple more. There'd been an attempt by Lucius and Sklarah to soak a blanket in fire oil and ignite it, and this provided some light and warmth against the frost globe effects, but didn't do any damage to Skitterlings. At one point amid the chaos, someone's crit meant they killed a foe and another Skitterling accidently blasted one of its own. Odds were beginning to swing in favor of the party.

      But it would prove to be premature to celebrate. Valavanora got off a shot with her bow, but (failure) missed, and was hit by (die of Fate 1) two lightning attacks that catapulted her back a dozen feet and knocked her to -2HP, dying. Tension ratcheted up as Sklarah--not wanting to risk using quicksilver since she was still beyond her limit--charged and (partial success) cut down a Skitterling, but was unable to reach the others before the next round. Niff (success) picked off a Skitterling with his bow, shooting through the net.

      The next round had the Skitterlings blasting away with lightning, and pretty much everyone but Niff went down with partial successes in avoiding damage. This is where Conditions made a huge difference. We were left with Arian Dazed, Sklarah Out, and Lucius Knocked Down. The Lion was Very Slowed from the frost globe, half-frozen. Everyone was low on HP. Niff was (failure) unable to tear the chain-net free, and the remaining Skitterlings were far enough down the corridor from the intersection that they weren't viable targets. The last shot by Arian had killed the third-to-last Skitterling, but two were still up and leveling their guns at Lucius and Arian for the coup de grace. Valavanora was dying or dead, Sklarah unconscious.

      Meanwhile, Nunk was busy clearing the rubble out of the rockfall, and G and A were frantic with the realization that their little subplot twist might have doomed the others. Their characters would arrive too late to enter the fray.

      It was at that point when M asked who was able to do anything, and I had her roll for the Lion to make a Heroic Effort. If it had failed, it would have been One Hurt Lion, which I'm sure would have made everyone sad, especially M. But the Lion (success) managed to leap on one of the Skitterlings and kill it. And EM realized her Peregrine Falcon Pet, Dera, was still functional, so we had her roll for the falcon, and she rolled a clean success. The falcon distracted the Skitterling enough for the Lion to get another attack, which it successfully pulled off. As Arian shook off her Dazed state and Lucius wearily climbed to his feet, the battle was over.

      Immediately, everyone raced to Valavanora's side. For some reason, no one thought about healing potions. I think they still had some stashed away, but no one has been super detailed in tracking their stuff, so maybe not. Or maybe Valavanora had charge of them, and no one thought to go through her belongings. In any case, it was clear she was dying or dead. Lucius attempted to Cure her wounds, but (failure) the God refused to heal a "woodland heathen." Undeterred, he tried his Heal skill and rolled a success, stabilizing her.

      I asked EM to roll for Valavanora to defy death, per John's suggestion above. I'd already narrated the atmospheric spiritual image of her standing before Death's Great Door in the Barren Forest of Mists, death's pale visage and bone white stag horns. Suspense in the group was high as EM took the dice and (snake eyes!) totally failed to Defy the Pale Horned One.

      Collective gasp. Valavanora was dead!
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nHexabolic \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n edited November 2012
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n At this point, everyone was chattering with nervous excitement. A was saying her Necromancer should have been there to intercede with Death, and she could bring Valavanora back to her body or maybe another body, like her falcon. EM thought both of those options would be cool, and seemed fine with her druid dying. All in all, her reaction was really stellar, and more grounded and positive than many adults I've played with (and me too on a few occasions in the thankfully distant past).

      Still, I reiterated that character death was only an option if voluntary, and Valavanora could opt to come back changed in some fundamental way from her brush with Death. They liked the idea of Death asking for Valavanora to fulfill some task, and I suggested her hair might have turned white from the experience. Everyone loved that idea, and M leaned forward and exclaimed to EM, "Do you realize how much depth you've just gotten from this?" What was so terrific about this entire interaction was the positive, supportive aspect to it all. The girls (and F, though he'd had to leave early and wasn't there for this bit) really impress me as a great group of players. I wish I'd had this sort of group when I'd started back in the White Box era.

      EM decided Valavanora would live, her hair white (but only on the front, like X-Men's Rogue, as JM pointed out that her thief Mist already had white hair). We're also still talking over the deeper change to personality and maybe some kind of death resonance in her Druidic magic. She's pretty excited about the potential story to be had, and she's talking about Valavanora's background and homeland, which is a win, as far as I'm concerned.

      A, playing the Necromancer, is thrilled at the potential for the two players to collaborate. Their imaginative play together outside of gaming features a lot of Necromancy lately. I expect a bunch of creative energy from the two of them on this piece.

      So...stabilized by the skillful minstrations of Lucius, Valavanora returns to Life with 1HP. We'll see later if Lucius's god will punish him for intervening where the god had refused to do so.

      All in all, everyone seemed pretty happy. We're taking a break over Thanksgiving weekend, to the disappointment of everyone, but I think it'll be good to have a bye, if only so I can focus on Nanowrimo and put down some more thoughtful notes about the world and an umbrella quest for them. I have a feeling that once out of the dungeon, the group will be ready for more story-focused adventuring. By that I mean less in the way of old school set-pieces and more opportunities to roleplay and solve mysteries, etc.

      Next: Thoughts on house rules and encumbrance, and my partial success therein.

      \n Thanks. We (EM and I) are noodling over how she wants the Death Aspect to manifest. She likes the idea of a personality effect, giving her the opportunity to do some pre-teen dramatic brooding. I've suggested an Autumnal resonance to her magic, like dried leaves appearing in her long hair, maybe colored by circumstance (blood red in combat, yellow when she's nervous, and so on). I am also thinking the Aspect manifests by allowing her to summon spirits normally inaccessible to Druids, not full-on Necromancy, but Death-Aspected Nature Spirits, like the ghosts of wolves, a Dead Wind, or a Winter Gloom--Winter and Autumn would feature strongly here.

      I've been playing that Necromancers get a +1 when dealing with spirits related to Death generally, but they can only summon those spirits. Inspired heavily by Garth Nix's Sabriel books (which feature one of the neatest, most evocative magic system evar), A has talked about peering past the veil of Death, etc. The Druid shouldn't step on the Necromancer's toes, but this Aspect may give her more power over those darker and forgotten aspects of Nature. At the same time, she could have a harder time using Spring and Summer Aspected magic. And I'm thinking maybe her falcon familiar now has a snow-white feather marking its brow.

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n So, house rules...mixed success here. Conditions worked really well, and the girls (and F) grokked them immediately. Their only complaint was that they wanted more conditions on the character sheet, and suggested a bunch I had considered but hadn't put down, stuff that in retrospect is obvious, like Dazed. JM later commented with the Dungeon Kids that she wanted Drunk as a Condition (having read a LOT of Tintin and Asterix, she thinks her characters being drunk would be funny). I am not putting Drunk as a Condition for these kids groups, though. Call me old-fashioned, but I'm mindful of the age-appropriate aspect here and parental sensibilities. I did rework the sheets for the Dungeon Kids to add a bunch of blank Conditions to be filled out in play.

      LIGHT
      Illumination was easy. I started with the idea that a torch provided light for three people, or a circle with a three-person diameter. Beyond that, light was ((dim)) for another three-person distance, giving a -1 penalty to perception. Beyond that, it was ((shadowy)) for a -3. Beyond that it was ((dark)) for -5, if you even got to roll at all. Different light sources had different distances. A candle gave light for one person, a lantern for five. A bullseye lantern projected in one direction only in a cone five persons long. A candle stub provided light only for examination of something up close. A torch stub worked like a candle. I assigned durations to each light source as well, using "turns" as an abstract, flexible unit of time.

      The girls took to these rules quickly, so there wasn't a problem.

      For rations, I just played that by telling them now and then they had to consume a ration or become Hungry. Ditto with water, which I'm tracking separately along with the Thirsty condition because they're in a desert realm.

      Encumbrance-wise, I overbaked the rules and ended up Beta-testing them on the girls, which wasn't the best idea. I'd asked about it ahead of time, and the girls seemed happy to have more structure in organizing their stuff. What I came up with, in tandem with adding Conditions and Illumination rules, was just too confusing and overwhelming.

      ENCUMBRANCE
      Taking inspiration from the BW thread, I started with the idea of a "thing" as the basic unit of "stuff" and "slot" as the basic unit of space.

      One thing takes up one slot unless ((tags)) change the relationship. So basically, you can add ((tags)) to either things or slots to change their space or add Condition-like aspects. The ((long)) tag, for example, basically means you can't carry it in an interior slot of a pack, bag, or satchel, but you can carry it in something ((large)) or ((unlimited)). The latter, incidentally, applies to the Satchel of Holding the Druid carries. The ((bundle)) tag means you can pack X units of the item as one "thing," so rations ((bundle 7)) would mean seven uses of your rations take up one slot. Coin would be too, but I pretty much just said a thousand silvers equal 1 thing and left it at that.

      Examples of Thing Tags: ((large)) ((small)) ((long)) ((bulky)) ((bundle X)) ((compact)) ((soft)) ((heavy)) ((fragile))

      Examples of Slot Tags: ((spacious)) ((hard)) ((cramped)) ((stretchy)) ((outside)) ((unlimited)) ((secure)) ((large)) ((scabbard))

      I went with each character having three torso slots and a Belt slot. A Pack has six slots and takes two Torso slots, Armor takes one Torso slot, a Satchel has two slots and takes one Torso slot. A Belt has two ((scabbard)) slots and can hold 10 ((small)) items in pouches.

      Then for Encumbrance, I started with the idea that each character can carry Things equal to 5+STR+CON without being encumbered...anyway, here it is.

      LESS THAN/equal to RATING = NO PROBLEM!
      MORE THAN ENC = 1 STEP SLOWER
      MORE THAN ENC +3 = 2 STEPS SLOWER +TIRED
      MORE THAN 2X ENC = VERY SLOW +HUNGRY

      As I said, it was too much and too fiddly, requiring lots of attention to tagging damn near everything in the game. M, who is an enthusiastic, attentive player and totally candid, commented that she found the encumbrance rules confusing, so I pretty much dropped them on the spot.

      For the Dungeon Kids, I reworked the sheet to simply say, here's how much space you have to carry your stuff. The rules are: (1) if you can write it in there, you have room (this is a real limitation for third graders, I have to point out); and (2) if a thing obviously wouldn't fit in a space--like a spear in a satchel--you can't keep it there; (3) outside slots can hold those long, awkward things; (4) if you're carrying a ton of stuff, you will be one or two steps slower, and you might get Tired and Hungry.

      NEXT: Dungeon Kids and the Huge, Incredibly Scary Bugbear.

      \n Alright, onto Dungeon Kids.

      We had C playing Romrama the Imperial Dwarf, JM taking the roles of Mist the Thief and Amber the Wizard, and S playing Lostariel the Assassin and Rhavaniel the Ranger. I'd confirmed with C's mom that C was coming, and she said he's been bouncing with excitement about the game, so that was reassuring. Also, when C failed to show a couple of sessions back, JM experienced a "big feeling" that involved tears and a face session with the living room couch pillow. I was happy not to have to deal with that.

      However, C arrived about a half hour late, and the kids were revved up with excitement. Basic management took a lot more effort this time around, and I had to pull out a bit of the Dad energy to get the S to sit up rather than lie on the bench, and to ask JM not to interrupt the other two players constantly with entertaining but totally off-point observations. I finally had to step back and ask if they wanted to go do something else for a bit or play the game.

      "Play the game!" they said.

      OK, good. We proceeded. Looking back on it, I am considering using Awesome Points more aggressively, in the way Faery's Tale suggests for Essence: as a reward encouraging good kid player behavior. It's basic self-preservation; if I have to expend so much energy on kid management, it'll suck a bunch of it away from the game while making it less fun for me. They need to pay enough attention to engage respectfully and interact with the fiction at the table. At the same time, they're third graders. There's only so far I can take my behavioral expectations before they become unrealistic. I'm betting some social defrag time at the start of the next DK session will help them settle down, plus a couple of planned breaks to let them get their ya-yas out periodically. It's a really different energy and pace from the Dungeon Girls group.

      Last session, they'd destroyed a goblin patrol led by an Orc Captain and accompanied by an Orc Drummer and Orc Shaman. We'd ended with EM's cliffhanger moment of her Wizard fainting from excessive quicksilver use, and then being kidnapped by the Orc Shaman's darkness spider spirit. This session, the party was setting out to rescue their captive friend.

      Next: Deeper into the Citadel of Evil.

      \n The kids finally settled down enough for us to begin. I'd also handed out the upgrade character sheets with the conditions. It was a bit much to start third graders with, but they handled it well. Would have been a lot easier if JM and S weren't playing two characters each. C wanted to make another character, but I encouraged him to stick with Romrama for the moment.

      The party gathered themselves and entered the next room, which was the room where the goblin patrol had barracked. I described a messy area with scattered blankets and half-eaten, flyblown food, a barrel of brackish water, and a table laden with more mess. Under the table lay a sleeping goblin with a jug in one hand.

      "What do you d--" I began, but C shouted, "I run over and hit him with my axe!" Almost immediately, S said her Assassin was running over to stab the goblin with her knife.

      "Umm, really?" I asked. "He's asleep..."

      They would not be deterred. I had to think hard for a moment about whether I was going to engage in any normative guidance here. I decided to let things play out as the kids drove it, but to introduce some small, related consequence down the road that might give them pause the next time they encounter a helpless victim. Certainly their current body count was nothing to sneeze at, so maybe I needed to let go of squeamishness and embrace the OSR ethos. But on the other hand, I felt discomfort at what encouraging what appeared to be classic murderhobo behavior.

      Anyway, Romrama and Lostariel hacked and stabbed the Bejesus out of the poor little bugger, and JM described her hardass Thief Mist coming over and kicking the corpse. I could see JM wasn't feeling kosher about this particular killing. JM told me later, "It didn't feel right. It didn't feel right to just go over and stab him in the heart." He wasn't doing anything and couldn't fight back.

      Mist kicked the body, JM says, "because she didn't want the others to know Mist felt a soft spot for the goblin." That statement fit right in line with my observation. JM says Mist didn't try to stop them because she was lost in thought, thinking about Amber, and because the other two characters just ran over there really fast. She thinks they could have gotten some information out of him if they hadn't been so quick with the killin'.

      They didn't find more than a handful of silvers in the room, so they quickly vacated it. The next room contained a staircase to the next level. The party set the order of march and ascended the stair.

      Next: The Bugbear!
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nHexabolic \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n edited November 2012
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n As it happened, the Citadel of Evil 1-page dungeon I'm using with the DK had some very convenient features on the next level. The write-ups were pretty light, a short paragraph per room, and I'd been populating pretty much off the cuff, taking inspiration from the text where it struck my fancy. The goblins and the orcs were entirely my own invention, for instance. But the upcoming level--and one of the neat things here is that they're working their way up to the citadel from the caverns below--described cells for prisoners, a guardroom with guards, and a shrine room where a sacrifice was taking place. So I determined that Amber had been taken to be a sacrifice. The Orc Shaman wanted to summon another, more powerful spirit to take out the party, since Amber's lightning spirit had burned right through his darkness spider. He was using a sacrifice because he didn't have enough quicksilver to call this thing (maybe uber-powerful spirits take multiple quicksilver doses, or failure on a dose is catastrophic). I also decided the guards were on alert.

      The guardroom described two hobgoblins and a bugbear captain, so it made sense to beef these guys up a bit. I gave the hobs 1 Armor, 10HP, 1d6+1 damage, and Keen Senses. The Bugbear ended up with 2 Armor + a Shield for a total of 3 Armor, 20HP, 2d6 damage, and Fearless. I thought we might see a pitched battle in the narrow corridor, and the real question was whether the party could save Amber from being sacrificed. If Amber was in jeopardy, we'd still apply the voluntary death rule, but there would be consequences. I was pretty sure JM would be OK with that.

      Stealth rolls failed as Romrama came into the hallway from the stairwell. One of the hobgoblins stationed at the other end of the corridor on the far side of the cells spied him and sounded the alarm. The party charged! Mist drew her elven Sword of Tirendel, which flashed blue for the goblinoid presence. She rolled for the sword's Cause Fear to Orc-kin skill, but though the hobgoblins were indeed afraid, they (failure) were able to brace themselves to fight. Their fear made them hesitant (-1 damage and would not pursue aggressive tactics). Mist and Romrama (both successes) took down the first one almost immediately, without harm to themselves. Lostariel the Assassin and Rhavaniel the Ranger (both succeesses) hurled daggers into the second one that caused him to stagger back from the pain of his wounds. So far, so good. Mist (partial success) dispatched the second one with a thrust of her elven sword at the top of the next round, but not before Romrama took a blow that forced him back a step. His armor, shield, and Tough ability kept him safe from damage.

      He actually is a really tough sucker, this Imperial Dwarf. Four points of Armor is nothing to sneeze at, which was good because he was still first level.

      Before the second hob died, Lostariel failed her attack, and S said she wanted the Assassin to be taken just like Amber, so I shrugged and described the darkness spider reaching down from the ceiling and snatching up Lostariel. Another sacrifice for the mill.

      Then the Bugbear stepped up in his lacquered armor, his scarred wooden shield, and his mace. I pulled out the narrative mojo with him, detailing his size, hairy ferocity, fangs, and most especially that heavy iron mace with its head cast in the shape of a snarling wolf's head. The kids' eyes got big as I pantomimed the Bugbear advancing, mace striking sparks from the stone wall and play-roared, "RRAHHR!"

      "What does Mist do?" I asked JM, since she had the high DEX and was first up. "He doesn't seem affected by the sight of your sword."

      "I...I parry the mace with my sword," she said. "And try to get out of the way. I'll, uh, I'll duck down low and roll between his legs."

      Romrama? C replied, "I turn and run. I'm going back to the stairs!"

      Rhavaniel? "I also back away, ready to run."

      Resolution. Romrama (partial success) successfully flees, but the sweeping blow of the Bugbear's deadly mace strikes the dwarf's trusty axe from his hand! Mist (critical) deflects the mace away from her as she rolls under the Bugbear and comes up behind him. For her crit special effect? "I hit him with my sword in the butt as I pass under him!" She deals 4HP damage after armor. In the Bugbear butt. JM chortles at this, it being right in line with high third grade humor. Had the Bugbear farted, we'd have had to take five due to hilarity.

      This swashbuckling strike (Die of FATE low) totally enrages the Bugbear, igniting a berserker fury. He wheels on Mist, spit spraying from his mouth as he shrieks in fury and brings his Mace down in a mighty overhand strike!

      Mist (success) leaps back from him and takes off down the corridor (the opposite direction from Romrama and Rhavaniel. At this juncture, I was a bit surprised that this group was running like this. Fortunately for her, Mist had, at the beginning of the session, given her light armor to Romrama, who (partial success) managed to modify the armor to fit him well enough to use. So she is as fleet as can be with Athletics and a +3 DEX. She easily outpaces the crazed Bugbear.

      Down that corridor is the iron double door to the shrine and sacrifice. I entertain the vision of an epic confrontation with cultists, Orc Shaman, raging Bugbear, and Mist alone for a round or two. But she bolts right past that door to the end of the hall, which is sealed by a closed door. A locked one, as the Die of Fate would have it.

      Mist frantically tries to jimmy the lock while the Bugbear crashes down the corridor toward her, and she (success) manages just in time to slip through and start up the wooden stairwell on the other side. Below her, the Bugbear squeezes through the doorway and roars at her before continuing its pursuit.

      Meanwhile, Romrama and Rhavaniel have gathered their courage and are racing after the Bugbear to help their friend. Will they be in time?

      Next: Final throwdown with the Bugbear of Doom and Ritual Interruptus.

      \nSidenote: I was surprised the characters ran from the Bugbear. Up to this point, in all the sessions I've run of WoD, I have never had a character outright flee (well, not including Romrama, who ran from the first batch of goblins, but I wonder if he's coming from his experience in adult D&D games, which might be less tactically forgiving). But none of the other characters in either group have outright bugged out. I asked JM later why Mist ran, considering Mist is a total badass (I didn't use that term with her) and could have taken it.

      "Well, Daddy, the way you described the Bugbear, it seemed so fierce, I didn't think I could take it."

      \n Aww, clearly, you're doing your job right, if the kids are running away from your scary bugbear!

      I loved the butt-stab. And the Death Druid! Man, I can't wait for more of both. Keep it coming, please!

      \n
      \n
      \n Thanks as ever. I continue to be astonished at the enthusiastic reception here, as well as from the kids.

      Mist runs up the stairwell, frothing Bugbear in pursuit. After four flights of stairs, she (failure on a CON roll) becomes Winded, unable to run further or even attack for a round as she catches her breath. While Romrama and Rhavaniel labor to catch up, the Bugbear, also panting from the exertion, screams and charges, swinging its mace at Mist's head. Mist (partial success) dodges the mace, but fails to evade the Bugbear's rush. His shield slams her over the rail for a four story fall!

      However, agile Mist drops her elven sword and (Athletics crit) grabs the rail to vault around and kick the Bugbear "right in the eyes, Daddy! With my heels!" Blinded, the Bugbear roars and flails wildly with mace and shield. Romrama arrives to (success) slam his axe deep into the monster's knee while Rhavaniel (crit) jams her magical hunting spear through the creature's brisket! As they wrench their weapons free, the mortally wounded Bugbear groans and staggers, then topples over the rail to land with a huge thump at the base of the stairwell, where it lies still.

      Mist finishes catching her breath, while the relieved players woo-hoo and exchange high-fives. Ever the practical one, Rhavaniel immediately suggests looting the monster's corpse. This they do and find a few semi-precious jewels. The mace, though cool and attractive, turns out to be heavier than anyone wants to carry. They leave it behind and proceed to check out the mysterious double doors they'd passed during the pursuit.

      Do they arrive in time to stop the ritual? Die of Fate says...4, which I interpret as being "sort of yes." They come down and Mist listens. She hears chanting. Shedding any pretense at caution, Mist kicks the doors open (they were unlocked) and charges the line of four cultists she finds standing between her and the Orc Shaman, who is about to lower Amber into a mysterious pit set between two braziers burning with green flames. Romrama follows on her heels and sees the chain binding Amber leads to a crank off to the right manned by a fifth cultist. A sixth cultist stands guard over a couple of other prisoners, including the captive Lostariel. Everything is in place for a set-piece climax.

      Or, as events unfolded, an anti-climax. Mist (success) slams her way through the cultists and leaps for the Orc Shaman, her elven sword held high. The Shaman (success for the sword's Cause Fear in Orcs and Goblin-kin skill) quails in fear of the eldritch blade as he frantically tries to pull out a magical fetish to call a spirit to his defense.

      Romrama (partial success) hacks down the cultist, but his blow carries through and cuts the chain, dropping Amber into the pit as the chain rattles off the spool. Rhavaniel (success) throws her spear into the cultist guarding S's other character, but he doesn't go down immediately, hanging onto life with 1HP as well as her transfixing magical spear.

      The next round, Mist kebabs the Orc Shaman with a crit that does ridiculous damage. JM demonstrates for us how quick the sword thrust is: "in and out *wssht* and he's SO dead." After the murderhobo killing of the sleeping goblin, this feels clean and surgical in comparison.

      Romrama drops his shield and axe and (success) grabs the chain as it leaves the crank spool, arresting Amber's fall. But now there are four cultists remaining. Two go for Romrama and two for Rhavaniel. Rhavaniel (success) takes one down with a thrown dagger, then runs over to grab Lostariel's staff, which is propped against the wall. Romrama (partial success) whips the chain he's holding around the first cultist's neck, but suffers a ferocious dagger slash from the other one, who rolls maximum success for 6 points!

      Meanwhile, Mist drops her elven sword and catches Amber, pulling her free of the pit.

      Remember how Romrama is a tough bugger, even at first level? He doesn't have his shield, but his Armor 1 and Toughness drop that damage by half, and he has 7HP, so even though the dagger cuts him painfully, he survives. The next round, he draws his own dagger and (success) stabs the cultist in the heart. With a +2 STR, he can deal out a heavy blow.

      Rhavaniel (crit) beats the last cultist down with a whirling staff move, and it's all over but the XP awards and loot. However, we only handle the first, since the kids are totally wound up. Mist is encroaching on 4th level, but Romrama reached 2nd, to general jubilation.

      This session saw a ton of Awesome Point use, and the kids were eager to use them to help one another. I started asking them how they could rationalize helping their friend. Was it a memory of a conversation (i.e., a flashback)? Do you give them a knowing look or a hand signal? Toss them a weapon? That helped them get a bit of a feel for how their characters interacted. But their unstinting generosity was touching. There was no hesitation, and they depleted their stores helping boost a roll all the way from, say, a partial at 7 to a full success at 10. Really cool teamwork, and I suggested they could get Awesome Points if they worked as a team. But they couldn't take off in the middle of a melee to make a plan. Plans had to be made when their characters had time to talk. They seemed cool with this limitation, and I released them to play until the moms arrived.

      As a final note, my wife, an elementary school teacher at the school attended by all the kids and girls except for EM and A, told me last night she had another DK player. A boy, J, whom she described as nice, really smart, gentle, a bit squirrelly, a fan of fantasy, and a newcomer to the school who has had a hard time making friends. I don't recall how it came up, but she heard he loved fantasy and played WoW, so she asked if he'd be interested in Dungeon Kids.

      "Is it a computer game?" he asked. No, she responded. It's a tabletop game with pencils and dice.

      "Oh. I've always wanted to play in a game like that," he said in a small voice. "Is it the kind of game that goes for awhile and then stops?"

      Well, she said, it's the kind of game that can go on for months or years. It lasts as long as your imagination lets it.

      "That sounds perfect," he said. "If I could play in a game like that, I think I'd be an Elf."

      His mom is onboard, so it looks like the Dungeon Kids have a fourth. Amazing.

      \n
      \n The streamlined Encumbrance rules worked great for the Dungeon Kids. Looking back on it, I definitely got caught up in overdesigning, making things granular beyond the simple framework of WoD. We didn't use the Illumination rules since this dungeon part was inhabited and featured plenty of torchlight.

      I was thinking about adapting the pack rules from the Story Realms game I playtested with JM a couple of months ago, where there are different kinds of packs from which you roll to pull out the stuff you need, maybe with a checkbox system of some kind to represent capacity. But for now, forget it. The Dungeon Girls love shopping anyway, so I don't want to take away any of their opportunity to spend their hard-won silvers on all sorts of widgets, pets, and extra gear.

      With four kids, I am considering going down to one character each. The problem here is that I really like both of JM's characters, Amber and Mist. And as JM pointed out, Amber is the only magic user in the party, but Mist is the only one who can Heal. Conundrum!

By contrast, S plays her Ranger and Assassin as identical, left-hand/right-hand characters, in perfect synchronization.

      \n
      \n Sounds to me like something you could resolve by talking it out with the group. Just tell them that now that you have so many players it's not necessary or desirable to have so many characters, but that perhaps some characters are so fun to have around or the players enjoy playing them that they should be kept, regardless. Complain that too many characters makes for extra work for the GM, too. And of course the extra characters can be kept as a reserve - players can switch between them between adventures or in case of fatalities, so it's not like they disappear altogether. Then let the group decide who, if any, are going to continue playing two characters. Perhaps they'll decide that everybody except JM really would rather just play one character. If they decide that everybody should have two, then tough break, streamline your methodology to handle the workload, and perhaps you might complain about it some more later.

      \n
      \n Thanks, sound advice. I'm not worried about my extra work, but the extra effort for them does distract them at the table, primarily from tracking their stuff and XP, and staying on top of their different characters in play. JM is pretty good at juggling her two and keeping them distinct, but it's challenging for her as well. I like the idea of their having a roster to pick from. We'll have an opportunity to talk about this at the next game. If they all want two characters, that's not a bad thing, as you've pointed out. They'll have to be more organized.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nEpistolaryRichard \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n edited November 2012
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Just to say that I've been greatly enjoying the thread.

      Regarding J, didn't you mention that the cultists had a couple of other prisoners? Could one of them be J's elf?

      Regarding JM's double characters, the happiest coincidence would be if J wanted a character that could fill one of those niches (either a magic user or a healer). If not and JM would prefer to retire Amber, then is a magic user really mandatory for the party? You can just dial down the magical threat for the next adventure. If JM would prefer to retire Mist, then I guess you really do need a healer, what you could do is have Mist imbue her healing ability into some kind of token (perhaps a necklace) and leave it with one of the other characters so they have Mist's healing power (only) plus whatever they were already bringing to the party.

      Regarding writing out the double characters in general, yeah, you can level with them about the logistics and that going down to one character will make it a better game, but to sell it to them I would look at hooking it into the fiction. Show them a fictional path where they 'retire' one character which they'll think is really cool. Some examples off the top of my head: they a message from one of their family requiring their attendance for some crisis; they receive a vision from their deity and are drawn to undertake a solo pilgrimage of some kind to gain new insight; their order/guild requires them to undertake an assignment of some kind or take additional instruction under the tutelage of one of their masters.

      Sell it to them as something that'll give those characters extra depth. Sell sending one of their characters off on a personal quest as a privilege for being one of the founding 'Dungeon Kids'.

      And then whatever it is you can then hook into future adventures - and hinting they may get an extra cool power (from their pilgrimage/tutelage/family) when that character returns.

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n\nHexabolic \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Thanks, much appreciated.

      We're thinking along the same lines about J's character being a prisoner. Way cool!

      Yeah, the double character issue may best resolve by rotating characters out in some way, and I like the idea of making the rotation awesome.

      My dilemma as far as JM goes is that I am a fan of both characters. Mist is a badass from the word 'go,' but Amber has taken on awesome story depth already. Part of that comes from the spirit-based magic system, of which I am growing more fond of every time I run the game. The choices of spirit domains and appearance add a huge dose of personality, and Amber has given the Goddess of the Moon, Travelers, and Hope her life, so she has the option to multiclass at the next level. I'm really excited by that possibility, plus spirits are incredibly fun to narrate. The sensawunda element they bring is high, much more flavorful IMHO than conventional fantasy spellcasting. Anyway, a rotation may be the best solution here.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nHexabolic \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2012 \n edited December 2012
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n So we've had a session each of Dungeon Kids and Dungeon Girls. I've some catching up to do. Dungeon Kids this last week turned out to be owned by the wizard, whereas Dungeon Girls finally saw the party leaving the dungeon and re-entering the world.

      I'll start with Dungeon Kids. C didn't show as his Mom forgot the date, and the girls didn't want to play unless he or the new boy J showed. Fortunately, J arrived with his dad, and after Junior Moppet and S gave him the tour of the house and played a little, we got down to business. The dad stuck around to watch, since he lived a good fifteen miles away and didn't have anything else to do that afternoon. I suspect he wanted to check things out since he didn't know me from Adam, even if my wife is one of his kid's teachers. But he was cool, and clearly enjoyed watching his son in action.

      J decided to be an Elf Archer named Ingor. He went with Athletics as a Class Skill, picked Stealth as his second, and from the Special Abilities Scout, Cantrip, Volley, and Skirmish, he went with Cantrip and Volley for his Lvl 1 package. For gear, he bought a dagger, a bow, some rations, and some torches. Simple.

      We started him off as a prisoner of the cultists freed after the battle in the sacrifice room. The game opens as the characters loot the dead cultists and find a chest. Mist opens the lock, and inside they discover a coffer and a couple of sacks.

      I'd found this site for help in generating treasure, and compiled a short list beforehand. (The results for potions, by the way, are pretty cool, since they include taste and color/viscosity as part of the random output.)

      Nothing terribly uber-awesome is in the treasure, and the girls let Ingor have the 25 gold coins (500 silver) because they feel he didn't have anything yet. There are a few potions as well as a ring of Resist Cold that Ingor also gets to keep. They decide to keep the Bugbear's scary wolfhead mace, which I'd determined was magical, but cursed: you have to roll a Con roll every day you carried it because it was just that heavy and tiring to pack around. Failure means you're whupped at -1 against your physical rolls until you can rest for a few hours and recover. Ingor is (success) vigorous enough to withstand the strain of the mace (for now)>. Mist considers taking it, but decides it's too heavy for her swashbuckling fighting style.

      After they finish dividing up the loot, the group heads out to explore the rest of the dungeon. Rather than take the stairs where they'd fought the bugbear last time, they go down a second tunnel that ends in a tall, massive iron door. I describe the door as bearing a snarling face cast into the metal. According to the thumbnail description in the mini-dungeon text, the door was mage-locked with a simple code to open it (say "NEPO"). Amber the Wizard calls upon her Lore and (partial success) remembers these doors often use simple codes like reverse words and so forth. I expected them to try some words and maybe decide to take the other way up via the stairs. However, the group didn't want to waste time guessing.

      Amber summons her night/cold spirit, The Book of Ice, and tells it to freeze that door! The Book, having appeared and opened to the page displaying its "How shall I serve you, Mistress?" greeting, proceeds to freeze hell out of the door. Amber rolls for effect and gets an 11. A thick layer of frost rimes the door as the spirit wraps it in supernatural chill so intense it causes the iron to creak. Then Ingor smashes the wolf-head mace into the door and (crit) smites the damned thing asunder! Shaking my head, I narrate how the frozen, brittle iron is broken apart enough for them to slip through it, but the shattering of the door also makes a huge racket. I am quite impressed at their strategy here, and proud of the use of Science! to solve the problem. Scratch one magic door.

      And this is going to be a problem, because the door opens onto a mountain path that leads around a curve and up a short distance to a citadel straddling the path. The hobgoblin garrison there, already alerted to the presence of deadly adventurers, is now aware that someone is coming. And they are ready.

      Next: A desperate scramble on the mountain path and...attack of the Frost Worm!
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nHexabolic \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2012 \n edited December 2012
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I'm not recalling all the details, but the party proceeds onto the path, which is about wide enough to allow two people to walk abreast. They go around the corner, see the citadel, and draw a volley of arrows from the hobgoblins.

      Everybody dodges. Mist (partial success) gets out of the way, but teeters on the edge of the precipice. Ingor also (partial success) dodges, but slips on some gravel and finds himself prone and sliding toward the edge of the path. Rhavaniel the Ranger gets clear, and sends an arrow into the throat of one of the hobgoblins before ducking back around the curve of the path. Lostariel the Assassin also gets out of the way but falls and slides nearly to the edge. Amber never ventured out, so she's perfectly safe.

      Things look grim. Mist (success) recovers her balance and cuts an arrow out of the air with her elven sword, which also (partial success) Causes Fear on the orc-kin hobgoblins and deters the next volley as the hobs suffer from a momentary panic. This passes as they recall their superior tactical position atop the battlements with a clear killing field on the path beneath them. It also means they'll target Mist more than the other characters.

      Ingor (partial success) uses his iron spike (I'd forgotten that was his other purchase aside from rations and torches) to secure himself on the path, but he can't get his bow out this round. Lostariel (partial success) grabs onto a handhold as she slips over the cliff's edge. She's unable to haul herself up, and (success with Decipher) hears the hob sergeant shouting orders for the garrison to loose another volley. She yells this to the party at large.

      Mist darts over and (success) hauls Lostariel to safety and (success) they manage to lunge around the curve of the path in time to avoid the arrows headed their way. Ingor rolls away, leaving his spike, and (partial success) gets back around the curve with only 2HP damage from an arrow grazing him.

      I picture everyone breathing hard and trying to figure out what to do next. Rhavaniel and Ingor pop out and pick off a couple more hobs, Ingor's falling dramatically off the battlement and tumbling down the cliff. The rest of the hobgoblins stick hard to cover after that. Another attempt by Rhavaniel to pop out and nail another defender (failure) draws a flurry of arrows. Rather than pull a hard move on Rhavanial directly, I decide the hobgoblins have gotten really confident. With dusk falling, their night sight makes them more at home. I describe the purpling sky and the the first stars appearing in the heavens as the party tries to figure out what to do.

      Then they hear the gate open and the portcullis being drawn up. The way I figure it, the hobs are going to try a sortie. Sure, maybe it's not the smartest thing to do, but it's getting dark, they know the party was in trouble and at least one of the invaders is wounded. Some of this is kind of a gift to the players. I don't know how they'd take the citadel--there are still over a dozen hobgoblins inside, and those walls are beefy.

      Now, I should take a moment to mention the book I found at one of my FLGS. It's Monsters, by WotC, a full-color, lavishly illustrated hardback book of typical D&D monsters, but geared toward kids. It was used, so I picked it up for five bucks. Both moppets found it fun to go through, but Junior Moppet in particular got into it, especially the Frost Worm and Purple Worm. She had been saying it would be so cool to fight a frost worm, and it was "so cute." (Yes, yes, she's an odd child with a quirky aesthetic.) When S had arrived, JM proudly took her through the book, and before long, both girls were excited about the prospects of fighting a 4,000lb. frost worm. So when they heard the portcullis grate open...JM exclaimed, "They're sending a Frost Worm at us! We get to fight a Frost Worm!"

      "Is that what you think?" I asked. "Really? A Frost Worm?"

      JM started chanting "Frost Worm! Frost Worm" and S immediately joined in, as did J a moment or two later.

      How could I refuse?

      "Yes," I said, with a rueful sigh. "It's true. The hobgoblins are likely too afraid to face Mist's elven blade, so they've released a monster to destroy you: a terrible, monstrous...Frost Worm!"

      "Yaaayyy!"

      "Woo hoo!"

      High fives went around the table.

      "This'll be so cool!" crowed JM. "Hey J, you'll be immune because you have the Resist Cold ring!"

      "Ah yes," I said after quickly perusing the Monsters book, "but you've forgotten the dreaded Frost Worm has more to it than freezy breath. You hear the terrible hypnotic trill come forth, slipping like icy snakes into your minds, coiling around your arms and legs, making it hard to move. Roll WIS to resist."

      Ironically, Mist (their best fighter) shivers and (failure) stiffens to immobility, captivated by the hypnotic trill. Ingor, Rhavaniel, and Lostariel (partial success) shake off the spell, but not for (die of FATE) two rounds. The Worm will almost be upon them by then!

      Next: Showdown with the Frost Worm.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nHexabolic \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2012 \n edited December 2012
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Almost everyone is down for the count, at least momentarily. But Amber (success) resists the insidious creature's charm. It's all up to her. Unfortunately, she's used up her Quicksilver quota for the day, and she's not a fighter.

      I decided then to try out an optional WoD magic rule someone posted. I sketched a pentacle on JM's character sheet and said, "You remember some of your Lore mentions that you can open your mind to the otherworld and summon spirits without quicksilver, Amber, but it costs you to do so. Each time you do it, you fill up a point of this star. When you fill up all five points, the magic will change you forever in some way. Do you want to risk the terrible--"

      "I do it," JM said. "That sounds So. Cool." I'd forgotten how much this kid loves adversity for her characters.

      Amber wants to summon a fire spirit to hit the Frost Worm, a blue fire spirit so it'll be the hottest kind possible, but there's no fire here for her to draw upon. Without some of the form, she can't summon unless she has a True Name. We talk a bit, I describe the high, bleak mountain path, the gloomy vista below, the starry sky above.

      "Can I summon a star spirit?" she asks. "They're fire, right?"

      "Hmm. Maybe. You still might need a name." I suggest she might know some constellations and star names via Lore. As it happens, (success) Amber does!

      She opens her mind to the otherworld and calls upon a blue star she'll name later. When it appears, bursting into serene brilliance on the path before her, she shields her eyes and (critical success) Commands it to "go make that Frost Worm really uncomfortable--burn it in the eyes!" With a critical, I might make it possible for her to learn more about it and make it part of her stable of spirits, but instead, I decide to give it a damage boost. She rolls 3d6 (fire against frost) and I double her result of 10. The spirit flies out and 'splodes into the Frost Worm's mandibled face for 20 points of damage! It is Blinded as well as hurt.

      Next round, the thrashing worm is shrieking, its trill stopped. Immediately, Ingor, Rhavaniel, and Lostariel snap out of it. Mist will snap out next round. The Frost Worm begins to slither down the path toward them. Ingor steps out to loose an arrow at it, and takes a blast of freezy breath. His ring (partial success) protects him, but his bowstring snaps. To J's credit, he took all the setbacks very well.

      Amber opens her mind again and this time summons an earth spirit from the mountain itself. A stone spirit. It appears, and this time she bargains with it, mostly because I think JM was interested in seeing how bargaining went. She asks it to collapse the path beneath the Frost Worm, and it agrees if the wizard in turn agrees to destroy the citadel polluting the spirit's mountain. Amber agrees, and (success) the spirit collapses the path on the cliff's edge, and the blinded Frost Worm topples over and falls to its doom.

      High fives all around. Frost Worm defeated! We were at a good stopping point by this time, so I called it. J had a great time, and his dad was really proud of him.

      I had a couple of insights from this session. First, I really have to skim down to one character apiece. Frequently, JM and S would say, "I do this!" and I'd have to ask "Which 'I'?" And they've been having trouble keeping their character sheets straight. Second, I'm not sure if the "open your mind to the otherworld" is too powerful. I can make Amber's first debility severe, but if it's super cool, will that still leave wizards fairly unlimited? If we were dealing with older players, I wouldn't hesitate, but the young ones might not care for "your right arm turns to willow wood" or "sunlight burns an exquisite calligraphy of pain into your exposed skin." But maybe JM would love this stuff, who knows?

      It'll be interesting to see how J and C work together. The elf archer and dwarf warrior make up that classic pairing.

      After everyone left, JM told me she wants to fight a Purple Worm next. "They're even bigger, Daddy!"
      \n

      \n That was your idea? Excellent! Yeah, I don't think madness plays as easily in the third grade set. I've played a lot of Ars Magica, though, so the notion of accumulating magical flaws and tells definitely appeals to me. They're flavorful and weird without being crippling.
      \n

      \n\nHexabolic \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2012 \n

      \n
      \n
      \n I'm also noticing the young ones really do better with about two hours of play, especially in larger groups. With just JM and S, they could play all afternoon, but when other kids are involved, the Playmobil box seems to generate a much greater pull. I suspect this is because JM and S are besties and spend time with each other outside of school regularly, whereas J and C are much rarer play opportunities.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nHexabolic \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2012 \n edited December 2012
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n So onto the latest Dungeon Girls session. I set up the campaign over at Obsidian Portal a week or so ago, and the girls just registered and started to populate it with content. I'll have some time over the holidays to put up stuff there. Elder Moppet spent some time last night registering her account at the site and making her avatar on Doll Divine. Watching her excitement, I feel like I've leveled up in some awesome Geek Dad prestige class.

      The game was short one player as L was sick and couldn't make it. I'd asked for snack contribution, because six pre-teens at the table turn out to be something like a pack of hungry dogs about an hour and a half in. This week, three players brought snacks, so we were basically rolling in food.

      We left off our last DG session in the aftermath of the battle with the Skitterlings and their lightning guns and frost globe grenades. The players started up by doing healing and divvying up the Skitterlings' weaponry. This was interesting to see who wanted what and how they allocated the energy vials for the lightning guns. It ended up being pretty egalitarian, though it took awhile because EM and A were doing a lot of interrupting. EM spends a lot of time at the table drawing and not paying attention, so she has to ask questions about what's going on. A seems so excited she can't help but interject off-point commentary in the middle of things. I finally had to stop talking until things settled down. I explained that I needed player attention so I could paint the scene and let us into the fictional space. After that, play proceeded more smoothly.

      It's been interesting to observe the different personalities and how they interact in the party dynamic. All the kids except A are a little self-conscious about roleplaying in dramatic fashion, but they're becoming more relaxed. There was some gentle banter going on among the group this week, and I didn't catch much of it, but it seems the players are beginning to gel. I suspect what's happening is the evolution of trust, which will let them engage more with the acting aspects of the game as they feel safe exposing their creative ideas to the group.

      M is very organized and forthright. She has her character sheets in a plastic folder, she shows up ready to play, and is engaged throughout the session. Of all the players, she's been the one quickest to offer compliments and feedback.

      F is a quiet but confident kid; he's been loosening up and starting to get into his character, which is fun to watch. His depiction of Niff is painting a picture of a tough, practical mercenary who focuses on getting the job done. At the same time, it's not the overbaked machismo so typical of middle school boys. He's starting to offer some tactical leadership in battle, but it's been very low-key so far.

      G is also sharp and engaged, and has been trying some roleplaying of her Paladin Arsalan (I've been misspelling it all this time). G has taken the role of note-taker, particularly with regard to treasure, and she and M seem to be assuming a bit more of a leadership role, at least in terms of kitten-herding the others. She and M are the main collaborators of the group.

      L is naturally very quiet, for all that she's the most seasoned gamer with her own polyhedrals. She is, however, paying attention and has contributed some great ideas. It's been fun to watch her comfort level grow, and the joyous expression on her face when she blew fire into the Wraith's face was priceless.

      A is the queen of drama here, and I mean that in a good way. She's a natural actor, and has been doing a lot of online gaming and some gaming with her family. She's also got a ton of backstory worked up, with her character's father basically being Voldemort for all intents and purposes. She does have a little trouble not interrupting, or knowing when not to try to shoehorn her story into the current scene, but her enthusiasm and creativity is inspiring.

      My daughter EM is actually kind of the bete noire at the table. She's often squirrely and seemingly more focused on drawing than on the game. But though she's been disconnected from time to time, when there's a combat, she's the one who comes up with a tactical choice that changes the battle in a fundamental way. She's been getting increasingly excited about the story and larger campaign, and we've talked about her behavior, so I hope to see more consideration going forward. In talking with her, she admitted she was feeling constrained and uninspired by the dungeon environment, so we'll see what the wider World of Dungeons has in store for her.

      Next: The prisoners of the three cells.

      Edited to redact some perhaps-too-personal observations of my daughter's table habits. It's tough shutting off the Dad mode.
      \n

      \n\nJohn_Powell \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2012 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n So. Awesome. Thanks again for posting!
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nHexabolic \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2012 \n edited December 2012
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n It's my pleasure, John. Thanks for the kind words.

      Taking up the Dungeon Girls narrative again, they decided to check out the cellblock Arian the Ranger had scouted on the last session, before the throwdown with the Skitterlings. The block contained three cells protected by stout, iron-bound wooden doors with Judas holes sealed by small latched covers. I hadn't prepped much for the game, and what I had prepped was treasure and monster stuff for a potential showdown with an Avatar of dread Klassh'Tuu. I didn't have the foggiest notion what or who was in these cells, but here I was, Johnny on the spot, so I turned to a prop for inspiration.

      A couple of years ago, I picked up a Harrow Deck from Paizo. This is a pseudo-Tarot deck of 54 trumps drawn from D&D imagery, with clever suit concept based on cross-referencing the nine D&D3E alignments with the six classic stats. Every card represents a particular alignment and stat, such as the Lawful Good Constitution card, etc. The art is decent, and some of the images are pretty interesting. Anyway, I drew three to give me some help for the cells, figuring they'd represent the cells from left to right, matching up with the position of the three doors.

      I drew The Paladin (Lawful Good - STR), The Teamster (True Neutral - CON), and The Crows (Neutral Evil - DEX). The Paladin shows the predictable: a noble knight armored in gold with a winged helmet. Cell #1 would have a captive Paladin, and he'd test their strength, either of their moral resolve or possibly by feat of arms. The Teamster shows a masked dwarf with a whip riding on the shoulders of a huge brute. I immediately had the idea that this was Nunk's master, Dimitrios Brog, an obnoxious slave master who used Nunk as a mount, servant, and freak show attraction. I thought he'd test the players' patience. The third card, The Crows, shows three crow-headed individuals playing a game of chance. I figured these would be three petty thieves from the city above: cunning, completely dishonest, and opportunistic in a malevolent way. They'd test the players' quickness and cleverness.

      Niff has Awareness, so he listens (partial success) at all three cells. From the first cell he hears what sounds like quiet prayer. From the second, whining and complaints about the food, the general situation, how unlucky Brog was, and so on. From the third come sounds of a quarrel, shoving and scraping and curses. Unfortunately, Nunk bellows something about how happy he is that his friends are safe, and Brog--the dwarf slaver--hears him. Immediately, Brog shouts orders to Nunk, stuff like, "Get me out of here, you worthless meathead! Hurry!" Some of the girls actually growl on hearing this. They've become quite protective of Nunk, and had been speculating about having a house and having Nunk be the housekeeper. I'd chosen a whiny nasal voice for Brog, perfect for grating on the nerves.

      Brog demands release and return of his property (Nunk). While Arselan and Arian argue with him, Niff picks the lock on the cell. Brog becomes a lot less aggressive once he sees the well-armed group outside. Nunk is cowed and afraid, and the players try to reassure him while Niff asks several times if he could just slap Brog. Arselan the Paladin replies no, that's not honorable. I describe how the scars Nunk has look like whip marks, and as Brog wails about how is he going to get around without his mount, the characters note how some of the marks on Nunk's shoulders look like callouses from a saddle strap.

      Outrage blooms around the table. However, slavery is legal in this city of Shardeej, and Brog claims he has papers to show his lawful ownership. Papers!

      Meanwhile, Niff (partial success) opens the next cell and releases the Paladin, who immediately kicks the door open and leaps out, seizing Niff's sword hilt and trying to draw it. Niff (success on STR) grabs the Paladin's forearm and prevents him from clearing the blade. During this moment of standoff, Arselan (success on CHA-Leadership) talks the fellow down.

      The Paladin, whose name turns out to be Bulat al-Mansour, is weak from lack of food. He explains how he was captured by the cultists and how grateful he is that the players have come in answer to his prayers. There follows a negotiation with Brog, and Andromeda the Necromancer finally offers to buy Nunk from him for 300 silvers. Brog immediately counters with 400, and I have fun hamming up a typical haggling session. After some back and forth, Andromeda proposes 360, and I rub my chin and then jab a finger at her and shout, "Done!"

      The players gasp, and Niff and Arian comment that they'd rather shank Brog (in essence) than acknowledge he has any claim on Nunk. Arselan and Burat want to free Nunk as well, and are inclined to simply declare him free, though they'd be in trouble from the authorities. Andromeda eventually convinces the others that buying Nunk would be the best way to achieve their goal without any legal trouble, and Arselan and Arian each put up a third of the purchase price. A disbelieving Nunk is told he's free, but he doesn't know what to do with that. Brog offers to buy him back for 100 silvers, and Niff finally slaps him.

      Next: The Crows and out of the dungeon into...an ambush!

      \n What fantastic thread! It's a joy to hear about these games.

      I'd love to hear about how the rules you are using have developed. What's the current state-of-the-art in the modifications you're using? How does encumbrance work? How do Conditions work? What's happening with the magical pentagram?

      \n\nHexabolic \n\n\n

      \n Thanks. Junior Moppet is the source of many amazing quotes from her quirky, powerful imagination. UserClone, your pony avatar would make my daughters totally squee.

      Yes, this is really the first full roleplaying encounter the group had, without a monster of one kind or another breathing down their necks. It was fun to offer the moral contrast between Brog and Bulat.

      Paul, I've cribbed a few house rules from here and there. Here are a few:

      AWESOME POINTS. We're using Awesome Points from Old School Hack, offered pretty much whenever someone rolls a crit or does something awesome. I tried the Other Worlds approach of handing everyone two chips to offer to other players as Fan Mail, so we'll see how that goes. One Awesome Point can be used to heal 1 HP, grant a +1 bonus to a roll, or make a small narrative statement about the immediate scene, such as "there's a lantern hanging from a pole at the edge of the plaza" or "these chains have a lot of rust and might have some weaker parts." So far, nobody's used them for this last bit. They've effectively been using their Awesome Points as a pool, so long as the holder can narrate some way in which they're helping the person who needs the points. You can spend as many AweP as you like in a round.

      THE PENTAGRAM. I just started this with Dungeon Kids. The original suggestion was to draw a five-point star on the mage's character sheet and allow the wizard to open his or her mind to the otherworld as an alternative to the other ways of summoning a spirit (e.g., quicksilver dose, hour-long ritual, magical contain). The cost is that if you fill up all five points of the star, you gain a Madness debility as a permanent feature of the character. Since Madness doesn't necessarily play too well with this age set, I'm opting for the magical warps or tells you'd see in Ars Magica. These would include things like eyes glowing when you get angry, a powerful smell of wet dog rising from you whenever you enter someone's home, a cool breeze following the direction of your gaze, your voice takes on a brassy echo, your fingernails grow super-fast, and so on. The challenge is to keep the warp something fun and interesting that a third-grader (especially) can keep in mind.

      Not totally sold on it yet, though. It may make Wizardly types too powerful.

      ENCUMBRANCE. I reworked the pack sheet to simplify things. Basically, you can carry whatever you can write in the pack space so long as it would make sense to fit into your pack (so no halberds, for example). The actual writing space constraints impose the limit. This isn't a group that needs to do extensive bean counting, and I got the feeling that a close tracking of encumbrance would prove a fun-killer. This isn't a huge deal with the Dungeon Girls because Valavanora has a Satchel of Holding.

      LIGHT. This has been fun. I posted these on a previous page, but basically, a torch lights up the space in a 1 person radius around the torch, beyond that it's Dim (sight awareness penalties), Shadowy (murky glimpses of movement), and Dark. Lanterns give more light, Bullseye Lanterns give more light in one direction. Torches and Lanterns also have burn times I can't remember here. These rules seem pretty intuitive, and the players have picked them up quickly.

      LUCK. I'm basically interpreting the Lucky Special Ability to give that player bonuses on the Die of Fate and the benefit of the doubt in luck-dominated situations. A single reroll per session seems too weak for a Special Ability. Mist took Lucky, though, and I need to remember it.

      PETS. So far, I've been letting the player roll with a +2 bonus for something in the Pet's domain. The Ranger's lion is good at Mauling the Beans out of someone, for instance. The Druid's peregrine falcon is good at getting all flappy-talony in your face. I may end up statting them up a little more to grant Skills and Special Abilities like Roar and Fly, letting the players run them directly as secondary characters.

      NARROW SKILLS. Magical items in the game or other special gear can grant narrow skills like Resist Fire or Climb Sheer Surface. These work like any other skill in the game for die rolling purposes, but within a more specific fictional domain. So you can't fail to Resist Fire when the dragon breathes on you--unless you need Resist Dragon's Fire--but you might be dazzled or have a hard time breathing if you roll a failure.

      SPIRITS. I've basically adjudicated the summoning of spirits you don't already have as Allies (like a magic user's two starting spirits) to require you have one of the desired spirit's Domains at hand. Alternatively, you need that Spirit's True Name. Otherwise, to summon a Fire Spirit, you need some kind of fire. The more you have, the stronger a spirit you can summon. Once the spirit arrives, you either have to bargain with it or Command it. If you don't have the Command Special Ability, you better be a good negotiator. The nature of the spirit determines how the bargain goes. My experience playing Werewolf: The Apocalypse gave me a lot of great ideas on how to play spirits (I remember our Glass Walker Theurge calling the spirit of Helicopter for an evac, using two torches to wave the spirit down to the LZ).

      I'll generally give more latitude and scope for spirit domain effects that aren't direct damage, but change the environment in some fashion. That's more interesting and rewards clever magic users.

      Basically, these two groups with a combined eleven players are working like a World of Dungeons Lab.

      I just had the Dungeon Girls meet to brainstorm backstories and world details, and they've taken off with the drama and orphan stories. Over at the Dungeon Girls Obsidian Portal campaign, they're filling in bios and other world content, making avatars, and so forth. Arselan's player listed Chocolate as an Item, saying you can't leave it out of your rations. :-) Arian (finally) named her Lion: Hodari, which means brave.

      \n These APs are amazing. I love reading them.

      I'm curious how you're doing group stealth rolls, since you seem to mention them a few times.

      It's one of those common problem areas in games - if everyone rolls and it just takes one fail for the group to fail, the odds of failure are super high.

      WoD's multiple success levels would lend itself to a good way to handle it, I'd think:

      Everyone rolls and counts successes.
      6- = 0
      7-9 = 1
      10-11 = 2
      12 = 3

      If, as a group, you don't get at least as many successes as the number of people rolling, it's a hard move against everyone who rolled 6-.

      This way, those who roll better can help balance out those who rolled worse and help even out the otherwise horrible odds of failure in a group roll.

      (It's sort of the opposite problem of the group Perception / Spot roll, where you want to have as many people rolling as possible because 1 success = total success.)

      LUCK. I'm basically interpreting the Lucky Special Ability to give that player bonuses on the Die of Fate and the benefit of the doubt in luck-dominated situations. A single reroll per session seems too weak for a Special Ability. Mist took Lucky, though, and I need to remember it.
      \nThis is super sweet!

      Another possible way to reinterpret Luck would be to allow a player to roll for things they wouldn't normally get to roll for, but with no bonus. (For instance, maybe you could roll for how well an allied NPC's attack works against your enemies.)
